`FIRMWARE_GUIDE.md`:

```md
# DMA Firmware Customization Guide

## 2026.6.18 Revised Edition

I despise scammers who sell **low-quality firmware** at absurd prices.

I also hate tutorials that deliberately skip the important parts, throw five screenshots at beginners, and then act like "change VID/DID" is firmware creation.

I wrote this for people starting from zero who still want to build **custom PCILeech DMA firmware** properly. The missing details are why so many copied builds never get past Device Manager.

> **Windows is the main route here.** Linux is useful for deeper capture and validation, but the build does not depend on `lspci`, `setpci`, sysfs, or Linux BAR dumps.

> This guide is intended for controlled lab use, hardware learning, interoperability testing, and security research. It is not a guide for bypassing access controls, anti-cheat systems, or protected environments.

> If the basics are already familiar, study **[VoltCyclone/PCILeechFWGenerator](https://github.com/VoltCyclone/PCILeechFWGenerator)** too. Automation helps, but the first failure will still force you to understand every setting it touches.

---

## 1. Before You Start

### 1.1 What You Need

You need:

- A Windows 10 or Windows 11 machine for Vivado and documentation work
- A separate Windows target/test machine if possible
- A real PCIe donor card
- A DMA FPGA board supported by `pcileech-fpga`
- Basic comfort with PowerShell, Device Manager, and editing text files
- Patience

You do **not** need to be a PCIe expert yet. But you must stop thinking that firmware means "change two IDs and press build".

### 1.2 What You Will Build

The work breaks down into:

- capture donor data on Windows
- set PCIe identity fields correctly
- configure BAR type and size
- generate shadow config-space COE data
- generate a WriteMask COE
- set DSN correctly
- avoid editing the wrong source file
- build with Vivado on Windows
- flash with the correct board-specific tool
- verify with Device Manager, Arbor, TeleScan PE, and cold boot
- recover when the firmware fails

### 1.3 What Counts as a Working Build

One clean Device Manager entry proves very little:

| Level | Result | What it proves |
|------|--------|----------------|
| 1 | Device appears in Device Manager | Basic enumeration only. This is not full success. |
| 2 | Hardware IDs, class, subsystem, BARs look correct | PCIe identity and resource layout are close. |
| 3 | Arbor/TeleScan readback matches donor plan | Config-space and capability structure are sane. |
| 4 | Command register and config writes behave correctly | WriteMask and shadow write path are working. |
| 5 | BAR reads complete and return planned data | BAR response path works at least for tested offsets. |
| 6 | Driver binds without Code 10/12/31 | BAR behavior, interrupts, PM, and config writes are good enough for that driver start path. |
| 7 | Driver operates correctly under normal workload | Basic driver binding is not enough; the driver must survive realistic reads, writes, interrupts, resets, and normal use. |
| 8 | Cold boot remains stable | Flash persistence and PCIe training survive a full power cycle. |

VID/DID-only work is an incomplete prototype. One enumeration proves only that the endpoint trained and answered enough config reads to appear. Without readback, config behavior is still unknown; without a cold boot, flash persistence and link training are still untested.

### 1.4 Windows Is the Main Route

The normal toolchain is:

- Device Manager
- Arbor
- TeleScan PE
- HxD
- PowerShell
- Python on Windows
- Vivado GUI
- Vivado Tcl Shell
- CH347 tools
- OpenOCD on Windows
- Vivado Hardware Manager

Linux stays optional and appears later as an advanced capture and validation path.

### 1.5 Important Corrections Before You Waste a Weekend

Read this before buying hardware.

| Bad advice from weak public guides | Correct answer |
|------------------------------------|----------------|
| "Use RTL8153 USB NIC as donor." | RTL8153 is USB, not a direct PCIe endpoint. Use a real PCIe NIC such as RTL8111, RTL8168, RTL8125, Intel 82574, or Intel I210. |
| "Only record VID/DID." | Not enough. You also need revision, class, subsystem, BAR layout, capabilities, MSI/MSI-X, DSN, driver version, and resources. |
| "`rw[203]` and shadow `rw[206]` are in `pcileech_pcie_cfg_a7.sv`." | Incorrect. They are in `src/pcileech_fifo.sv`. |
| "`rw[21]` is master abort enable." | Incorrect. `rw[21]` is Command Register auto-set. `rw[20]` is Status Register auto-clear. |
| "`pcie_7x_0_core_top.v` is a normal source file." | Usually wrong. It is a Vivado-generated IP output file under `.gen/...` and can be overwritten. |
| "Zero4K emulates any BAR." | No. Zero4K is 4KB. A larger BAR needs more logic or a smaller donor. |
| "One flash command works for all boards." | No. Squirrel, CaptainDMA, Enigma, ZDMA, AC701, and other boards may use different tools. |
| "Warm reboot is enough after flash." | No. Power off, remove power if needed, wait, power on, then verify. |

### 1.6 Sanity Check

Before buying parts or opening Vivado:

- You accept that this is a Windows-first workflow
- You have a real PCIe donor, not a USB device
- You know your DMA board model and FPGA part
- You understand that VID/DID-only work is not real firmware customization

---

## 2. Hardware Preparation

### 2.1 Choosing a DMA Board

Do not choose a board by random AliExpress name. Identify the actual FPGA part and package.

| Board family | Common FPGA | Common upstream folder | Common flashing path |
|-------------|-------------|------------------------|----------------------|
| Squirrel | XC7A35T FGG484 | `PCIeSquirrel/` | OpenOCD / Squirrel update port |
| LeetDMA 35T | XC7A35T FGG484 | `PCIeSquirrel/` | Squirrel-compatible or vendor tool |
| CaptainDMA M2 35T x1 | XC7A35T CSG325 | `CaptainDMA/35t325_x1/` | CH347 / M2 flash module |
| CaptainDMA M2 35T x4 | XC7A35T CSG325 | `CaptainDMA/35t325_x4/` | CH347 / M2 flash module |
| CaptainDMA 35T FGG484 | XC7A35T FGG484 | `CaptainDMA/35t484_x1/` | Vendor-specific |
| CaptainDMA 75T | XC7A75T FGG484 | `CaptainDMA/75t484_x1/` | CH347 / vendor tool |
| CaptainDMA 100T | XC7A100T FGG484 | `CaptainDMA/100t484-1/` | CH347 / vendor tool |
| Enigma X1 | XC7A75T FGG484 | `EnigmaX1/` | Vivado Hardware Manager or vendor flow |
| ZDMA | XC7A100T FGG484 | `ZDMA/` | Vivado/JTAG/vendor flow |
| AC701 | Kintex-7 board | `ac701_ft601/` | Vivado Hardware Manager |

Wrong board folder = wrong part, wrong pins, wrong clocks, wrong flash, broken firmware.

### 2.2 Choosing a Donor Card

Good beginner donors:

- Realtek PCIe NIC: RTL8111, RTL8168, RTL8125
- Intel PCIe NIC: 82574, I210
- Simple PCIe serial card
- Simple PCIe USB controller if you accept extra complexity

Bad beginner donors:

- RTL8153 USB NIC - not PCIe
- GPU - huge BARs, power management, firmware, driver handshake
- Wi-Fi/Bluetooth - NVM, calibration, firmware loading
- NVMe/RAID/HBA - queues, doorbells, MSI-X, stateful controller logic
- Anything with a giant BAR if you only plan to use Zero4K

### 2.3 Hardware You Should Have Before Editing Firmware

Minimum:

- DMA FPGA board
- Donor PCIe card
- Windows build machine
- Windows target/test machine
- Correct USB/JTAG/update cable
- Correct flashing tool for your board
- Known-good stock firmware or recovery image

Recommended:

- PCIe riser with external power
- Second USB cable
- Second PCIe slot/motherboard for testing
- Board vendor recovery instructions saved offline
- Photos of board silkscreen and FPGA marking

### 2.4 The Critical Flashing Rule

If you do not know how to restore stock firmware, do not flash custom firmware yet.

Before flashing custom firmware, confirm:

- You have stock firmware backed up or downloadable
- You know the board's update/JTAG/CH347 port
- Your PC can detect the board with the flashing tool
- You know the exact FPGA part and package
- You know whether your command loads SRAM or writes flash
- You know how to recover if the board stops enumerating

### 2.5 Recovery and Unbrick Preparation

Do this before the first custom flash, not after you panic.

**Programmer detection**

Check Windows Device Manager:

```text
Device Manager -> Universal Serial Bus controllers
Device Manager -> Ports (COM & LPT)
Device Manager -> Universal Serial Bus devices
```

You are looking for the programmer/update interface, not the PCIe endpoint:

- CH347/CH341 style device
- FTDI/FT2232 style device
- Xilinx cable / Digilent cable
- vendor-specific update interface

The programmer side is ready when:

```text
Programmer appears when the USB/update cable is connected.
Flashing tool can list or open the programmer.
```

Stop here if you get:

```text
Unknown USB device
driver error
tool cannot open interface
tool sees nothing
```

Fix programmer drivers before flashing. A board you cannot program is not ready for experiments.

**Stock firmware backup**

Before custom firmware:

- download stock firmware if vendor provides it
- save the original image if the tool can read it
- save the exact board folder and upstream commit
- save vendor recovery instructions
- save photos of switch/jumper/update-port positions

**Wrong image symptoms**

Wrong board image or wrong FPGA package can cause:

- target PC does not boot
- board no longer enumerates
- PCIe link never trains
- flash tool still sees programmer but PCIe side is dead
- LEDs behave differently from stock
- Vivado/OpenOCD reports unexpected IDCODE

Do not keep flashing random images. Go back to stock and verify board identity.

**SRAM load vs flash programming**

If the new firmware works until power is removed, you probably only loaded SRAM.

Cold boot exposes:

- SRAM-only loads
- bad flash programming
- PCIe training problems
- timing-sensitive startup bugs
- target BIOS slot negotiation issues

Warm reboot lies. Cold boot tells the truth.

### 2.6 Hardware Ready Check

Do not open the firmware project until these are sorted:

- Board model is identified
- FPGA part and package are identified
- Correct upstream folder is known
- Correct flashing tool is known
- Recovery path is known
- programmer is detected in Windows
- stock firmware or recovery image is available
- SRAM vs flash programming difference is understood
- Donor is a real PCIe endpoint

---

## 3. Windows Donor Card Analysis

### 3.1 A Donor Capture Is More Than IDs

Writing down VID/DID is not a donor capture. Firmware built from those two fields usually breaks as soon as Windows does anything beyond enumeration.

Minimum Windows-only donor capture must include:

- Vendor ID
- Device ID
- Revision ID
- Class Code
- Subsystem Vendor ID
- Subsystem ID
- BAR0-BAR5 type and size
- 32-bit vs 64-bit BAR info
- prefetchable flag
- Capability Pointer
- Standard Capability Chain
- Extended Capability Chain
- MSI or MSI-X details
- DSN presence and value if present
- Windows driver version
- Device Manager resources
- Device Manager events/status

### 3.2 Windows Capture Quality Levels

Windows tools provide enough data for the main workflow, but capture quality still varies. A few screenshots are not a raw dump.

| Level | Name | What you have | Verdict |
|------|------|---------------|---------|
| 0 | VID/DID only | `VEN_xxxx&DEV_xxxx` and nothing else | Not acceptable. This is not donor capture. |
| 1 | Device Manager basic | Hardware IDs, Resources, Driver tab, Events tab | Minimum identity evidence, still weak. |
| 2 | Decoded config | Arbor or TeleScan PE header/BAR/capability decode | Usable for beginner identity and BAR setup. |
| 3 | Full Windows decode | BAR0-BAR5, standard capabilities, extended capabilities, MSI/MSI-X, DSN, driver version | Real Windows donor capture. |
| 4 | Re-checkable raw-like data | Tool export, `.tlscan`, saved config bytes/table, or structured offset/DWORD file | Strong. You can regenerate COE and audit offsets. |
| 5 | Donor-target diff ready | Donor and target records can be compared field by field | Preferred Windows route. |

Straight answer:

- Level 0 is not acceptable.
- Level 1 is only a starting point.
- Level 2 can get you moving.
- Level 3 is the minimum for a serious Windows-only tutorial.
- Level 4 is where COE generation stops being guesswork.
- Level 5 lets you compare the firmware against the donor plan field by field.

### 3.3 Create the Donor Folder First

Create this folder before touching Vivado:

```text
C:/dma/donor/<donor-name>/
  device-manager-hardware-ids.png
  device-manager-resources.png
  device-manager-events.png
  device-manager-driver-version.txt
  arbor-export.txt
  arbor-config-space.png
  telescan-save-file.tlscan
  config-offsets.csv
  config-dwords.csv
  bar-layout.png
  capabilities.png
  extended-capabilities.png
  msi-msix.png
  donor_specs.md
```

What each file is for:

| File | Where it comes from | Why you need it later |
|------|---------------------|-----------------------|
| `device-manager-hardware-ids.png` | Device Manager -> Details -> Hardware Ids | Confirms VID/DID/Subsys as Windows sees it. |
| `device-manager-resources.png` | Device Manager -> Resources | Confirms Windows BAR resource ranges. |
| `device-manager-events.png` | Device Manager -> Events | Records driver install/start behavior. |
| `device-manager-driver-version.txt` | Device Manager -> Driver tab | Driver version matters for binding behavior. |
| `arbor-export.txt` | Arbor export/copy view | Human-readable config decode. |
| `arbor-config-space.png` | Arbor config page screenshot | Visual proof of offsets and fields. |
| `telescan-save-file.tlscan` | TeleScan PE save file | Re-openable donor capture. |
| `config-offsets.csv` | Manual or tool-derived offset/byte table | Lets you audit config-space offsets without relying on memory. |
| `config-dwords.csv` | Offset/DWORD table for COE generation | Input for `pcileech_cfgspace.coe`. |
| `bar-layout.png` | Arbor/TeleScan BAR/resource screenshot | Used for Vivado BAR configuration. |
| `capabilities.png` | Arbor/TeleScan standard capabilities | Used for shadow config and writemask planning. |
| `extended-capabilities.png` | Arbor/TeleScan extended capabilities | Used for DSN/AER/LTR/etc. |
| `msi-msix.png` | MSI/MSI-X capability page | Used for interrupt and BAR table planning. |
| `donor_specs.md` | Your summary file | The single source of truth for firmware edits. |

If you do not have these files, you do not have a donor capture. You have a guess.

### 3.4 Device Manager Capture

Install the donor card in Windows and let the normal driver load.

Open:

```text
Device Manager -> View -> Devices by connection
```

Find the donor device, then capture:

**Hardware IDs**

```text
Right click device -> Properties -> Details -> Hardware Ids
```

A complete Hardware IDs capture looks like:

```text
PCI\VEN_10EC&DEV_8125&SUBSYS_012310EC&REV_05
PCI\VEN_10EC&DEV_8125&SUBSYS_012310EC
PCI\VEN_10EC&DEV_8125&CC_020000
PCI\VEN_10EC&DEV_8125&CC_0200
```

What to extract:

```text
Vendor ID: 10EC
Device ID: 8125
Subsystem Vendor ID: 10EC
Subsystem ID: 0123
Revision ID: 05
Class Code: 020000
```

This is only a VID/DID capture:

```text
PCI\VEN_10EC&DEV_8125
```

This is incomplete. You still need revision, class, subsystem, BARs, capabilities, and driver data.

**Resources**

```text
Properties -> Resources
```

Record:

- Memory Range entries
- I/O Range entries if present
- Interrupt request
- Conflicting device list

Use this for BAR assignment and resource conflict checks.

**Driver**

```text
Properties -> Driver
```

Save:

```text
Provider:
Date:
Version:
Digital Signer:
```

Driver version matters. A different driver version can read different BAR offsets or require different capabilities.

**Events**

```text
Properties -> Events
```

Save install/start messages. Later, if your target has Code 10 or Code 31, this gives you something to compare against.

### 3.5 Arbor Capture

Run Arbor as Administrator.

```text
Right click Arbor.exe -> Run as administrator
```

Steps:

1. Open Local System
2. Scan or rescan PCIe devices
3. Find the donor by slot, name, or VID:DID
4. Open PCI config decode
5. Capture header fields
6. Capture BAR layout
7. Capture standard capabilities
8. Capture extended capabilities
9. Capture MSI/MSI-X
10. Export text if supported

Record these fields:

| Field | Example | Used for |
|-------|---------|----------|
| Vendor ID | `10EC` | PCIe IP identity |
| Device ID | `8125` | PCIe IP identity |
| Revision ID | `05` | PCIe IP identity |
| Class Code | `020000` | PCIe IP identity |
| Subsystem Vendor ID | `10EC` | PCIe IP or shadow |
| Subsystem ID | `0123` | PCIe IP or shadow |
| Capability Pointer | `0x40` | Capability chain |
| BAR0 type/size | Memory, 32-bit, 16KB | Vivado BAR config |
| MSI/MSI-X | MSI present, MSI-X absent | Writemask and BAR planning |
| DSN | Present or absent | `cfg_dsn` and shadow data |

Arbor is giving useful data when:

```text
Header decodes cleanly.
BARs have sane power-of-two sizes.
Capabilities chain walks forward and terminates.
Extended capabilities are readable if present.
```

Treat this as a capture or tool failure:

```text
Vendor ID: FFFF
Device ID: FFFF
BAR size unknown
Capability chain broken
Extended config unreadable
```

Do not continue until you know whether the donor capture is bad or the tool is limited.

### 3.6 TeleScan PE Capture

TeleScan PE is useful because it can save a re-openable capture file.

Steps:

1. Run TeleScan PE as Administrator
2. Scan local PCIe devices
3. Select the donor
4. Save the `.tlscan` file
5. Screenshot BAR layout
6. Screenshot capabilities
7. Screenshot extended capabilities
8. Screenshot MSI/MSI-X

Do not rely on one screenshot of the device name. Save the actual capture file.

### 3.7 Windows-Only Raw Config Alternatives

Linux raw config dumps are convenient, but they are not mandatory for this Windows-first guide. The Windows replacement is discipline:

1. Save a re-openable capture if the tool supports it.
2. Export text if the tool supports it.
3. Build an offset-based table from the exported data.
4. Verify offsets with HxD or a script.
5. Record the limitation if the capture is partial.

Acceptable Windows raw-like sources:

| Source | Strength | Notes |
|--------|----------|-------|
| TeleScan `.tlscan` save file | Strong | Re-openable and reviewable. Keep it. |
| Arbor text export | Strong if offsets are present | Good COE source if it preserves offsets clearly. |
| Tool screenshot only | Weak | Better than memory, not equivalent to raw data. |
| Manual `offset,dword` CSV | Minimum route | Usable, but easy to mistype. Verify aggressively. |
| HxD-opened binary/export | Strong if complete | Best Windows route if your tool exports bytes. |

Capture limitation examples:

```text
Capture limitation: only 256-byte standard config view exported.
Capture limitation: extended config present in screenshots but no raw 4KB export.
Capture limitation: BAR layout captured, but BAR contents not captured.
Capture limitation: MSI-X table offset known, but BAR table readback not tested yet.
```

Do not write "full donor capture" if you only have screenshots. Say exactly what you have.

### 3.8 HxD and Script Sanity Check for Exports

If you have an exported binary or hex-like file, open it in HxD and check:

| Offset | What to verify |
|--------|----------------|
| `0x00` | VID/DID bytes |
| `0x04` | Command/Status |
| `0x08` | Revision/Class |
| `0x10-0x24` | BAR registers if present |
| `0x2C` | Subsystem IDs |
| `0x34` | Capability Pointer |
| `0x100` | First extended capability header if present |

If the tool export cannot be mapped back to offsets, do not use it blindly for COE generation. A pretty dump with no offsets is how people build broken firmware and then blame Vivado.

### 3.9 Windows-Only Minimum Capture Checklist

If you never touch Linux, you must still have:

```text
[ ] Hardware IDs screenshot
[ ] Resources screenshot
[ ] Driver version saved
[ ] Arbor or TeleScan config decode
[ ] VID/DID
[ ] Revision ID
[ ] Class Code
[ ] Subsystem Vendor ID
[ ] Subsystem ID
[ ] BAR0-BAR5 enabled/disabled
[ ] BAR0-BAR5 memory vs I/O
[ ] BAR0-BAR5 32-bit vs 64-bit
[ ] BAR0-BAR5 prefetchable flag
[ ] BAR0-BAR5 size
[ ] Capability Pointer
[ ] Standard Capability Chain
[ ] Extended Capability Chain
[ ] MSI/MSI-X information
[ ] DSN present/absent
[ ] Windows driver version
```

If you are missing this, stop. Do not open Vivado yet.

Create:

```text
C:/dma/donor/<donor-name>/donor_specs.md

### 3.11 Donor-Target Diff Plan

Compare the target against the donor. "Looks close" is not a diff.

Use Windows tools:

| Diff area | Donor source | Target source |
|-----------|--------------|---------------|
| Device name and Hardware IDs | Device Manager donor screenshot | Device Manager target screenshot |
| Resources/BAR ranges | Device Manager Resources | Device Manager Resources |
| Config header | Arbor/TeleScan donor | Arbor/TeleScan target |
| Key offsets | `donor_specs.md` / CSV | Arbor/TeleScan target readback |
| BAR layout | Arbor/TeleScan donor | Arbor/TeleScan target |
| Capability chain | Arbor/TeleScan donor | Arbor/TeleScan target |
| Extended capabilities | Arbor/TeleScan donor | Arbor/TeleScan target |
| MSI/MSI-X | Arbor/TeleScan donor | Arbor/TeleScan target |
| Command/Status behavior | Controlled config write test | Controlled config write test |

Classify every difference:

| Category | Meaning | Example |
|----------|---------|---------|
| Stable field | Should match donor plan | VID/DID, Class Code, Subsystem IDs |
| Runtime field | Expected to change at runtime | Command, Status, BAR assigned address, MSI enable |
| Intentional difference | You chose it and documented it | Link width differs from donor hardware |
| Bug | Should match or behave correctly but does not | Capability pointer broken, BAR size wrong, VID/DID stale |

If you cannot classify a difference, do not hide it. Mark it unknown and investigate.

### 3.12 Review the Capture

A usable donor folder contains:

- `donor_specs.md` is filled out
- screenshots are saved
- capture quality level is declared
- capture limitations are written down
- driver version is saved
- BAR layout is known
- capability chain is known
- MSI/MSI-X is known
- DSN status is known
- raw-like export or manual offset table exists for COE generation
- donor-target diff fields are prepared
- you can explain what each BAR is supposed to be

If all you have is `VEN_xxxx&DEV_xxxx`, you are not done.

---

## 4. Windows Development Environment Setup

### 4.1 Windows Version Recommendation

Recommended:

- Windows 10 22H2
- Windows 11 23H2 or newer

Avoid:

- heavily modified "tiny" Windows images
- systems with broken driver installation
- paths under OneDrive sync
- non-admin user accounts for Vivado/tool installation

Use a normal Windows install. Do not create extra problems.

### 4.2 Required Tools

Install:

| Tool | Purpose |
|------|---------|
| Vivado | Build FPGA bitstream |
| 7 Series device support | Required for Artix-7 boards |
| Python 3.10+ | COE generation and parsing |
| Git for Windows | Clone repositories and track changes |
| VS Code | Edit SystemVerilog, Tcl, Markdown |
| PowerShell 5+ or PowerShell 7 | Run helper commands |
| Arbor | Windows PCIe inspection |
| TeleScan PE | Alternative Windows PCIe inspection |
| HxD | View/edit binary and hex data |
| CH347 tool | Flash many CaptainDMA-style boards |
| OpenOCD for Windows | Flash Squirrel/compatible boards when applicable |
| Vivado Hardware Manager | JTAG flash/debug for supported boards |

### 4.3 Vivado Installation

During install:

- Select Vivado
- Select 7 Series
- Select Artix-7
- You do not need every FPGA family

Verify:

```powershell
& "C:\Xilinx\Vivado\2023.2\bin\vivado.bat" -version
```

A working Vivado install prints:

```text
Vivado v2023.2 ...
```

The path or version is wrong if you get:

```text
The term 'C:\Xilinx\Vivado\2023.2\bin\vivado.bat' is not recognized
```

Fix the install path or version number.

### 4.4 Python Installation

Install Python from python.org or Microsoft Store, then:

```powershell
python --version
pip --version
```

Python is on PATH when you get:

```text
Python 3.10.x
```

Fix PATH if you see:

```text
Python was not found
```

Fix PATH before continuing.

### 4.5 Git Installation

Verify:

```powershell
git --version
```

Git should print:

```text
git version 2.x.x.windows.x
```

### 4.6 Path Rules

Use short ASCII-only paths.

Keep projects under paths like:

```text
C:/dma/
C:/dma/pcileech-fpga/
C:/dma/work/squirrel-rtl8125/
C:/dma/donor/rtl8125/
C:/dma/builds/
```

Avoid paths like:

```text
C:/Users/Your Name/Desktop/My DMA Firmware Test/
C:/Users/Name/OneDrive/Desktop/dma/
C:/Projects/DMA Firmware New Final Final/
```

Avoid:

- spaces
- non-ASCII characters
- cloud sync folders
- long paths
- protected folders

Vivado path bugs are real. Do not invite them.

### 4.7 Administrator Rights and Driver Problems

Run as Administrator when needed:

- Arbor
- TeleScan PE
- CH347 flashing tools
- OpenOCD if driver access fails
- Vivado Hardware Manager if cable detection fails

Common Windows driver problems:

| Symptom | Likely cause |
|---------|--------------|
| CH347 not detected | Driver missing or wrong driver |
| FTDI/OpenOCD cannot open device | WinUSB/FTDI driver mismatch |
| Vivado Hardware Manager sees no target | Cable driver missing, board not powered, wrong JTAG port |
| Arbor cannot read config | Not admin, tool limitation, device not active |
| TeleScan shows stale device | Rescan needed, device disabled, driver failed |

### 4.8 Verify the Toolchain

Check the toolchain once before cloning anything:

- Vivado launches
- 7 Series support is installed
- Python works
- Git works
- VS Code can open the repo
- Arbor or TeleScan runs as admin
- HxD is installed
- your board flashing tool can detect the board before custom flashing
